Chum Mey was eventually released and reunited with his wife and fourth child, but they were all killed by the Khmer Rouge soon after. The Theatre was the first purpose-built home for drama in Britain since Roman times and was where Shakespeare made his home after moving from Warwickshire to London. The Curtain was built about 100 yards south of The Theatre, and The Rose was opened before the Globe in 1587. The Globe theatre is located just down river of where Southwark Bridge now stands, and it's not to be confused with the reconstructed Shakespeare's Globe a few minutes away. Silver Street, Cripplegate, is where Shakespeare lodged with a refugee Huguenot family in the early 1600s, and a massive underground car park now lurks beneath London Wall on the same spot. Therefore, Answer (b) is better.  Here's my answer:  The UK's air pollution is a severe problem, with high levels of various substances such as nitrogen dioxide, sulphur dioxide, and ammonia. The pollution is mainly caused by road transport and residential emissions, and the problem is exacerbated by the high number of diesel vehicles in the UK. The World Health Organization recommends strict limits for exposure to PM2.5 and PM10, and the EU is taking legal action against the UK for persistently over the safe limit for air pollution. The problem is not limited to the UK, as most EU countries struggle to meet the targets, and it is responsible for 7 million premature deaths a year around the world. To tackle the problem, a shift towards less polluting forms of transport and low-emission vehicles is necessary.  Therefore, Answer (b) is better.

Here's my answer:  The UK's air pollution is a severe problem, with parts of the country reaching level 10 on the Department of the Environment's scale. The pollution is caused by a combination of factors, including dust from the Sahara, industrial pollution from Europe, and emissions from road transport and residential areas. The pollution is made up of various substances, including nitrogen dioxide, sulphur dioxide, and ammonia, which can have serious health effects, particularly nitrogen dioxide. The UK is persistently over the safe limit for air pollution, and the problem is not unique to the country, as most EU countries struggle to meet the targets. The World Health Organization estimates that air pollution is responsible for seven million premature deaths a year around the world, and in the UK, around 30,000 people die prematurely every year due to problems associated with air pollution. To tackle the problem, a shift towards less polluting forms of transport is necessary.  Therefore, Answer (b) is better.

My answer:  Prosopagnosia is a condition where individuals are unable to recognize faces, despite being able to see the individual features of the face such as the eyes, nose, and mouth. People with prosopagnosia may develop coping strategies, but these are not always reliable.  Evaluation:  Both answers provide a brief summary of the symptoms of prosopagnosia. My answer:  Prosopagnosia is a condition where individuals struggle to recognize faces, including those of familiar people. The condition can be developmental or acquired, with the latter being caused by brain damage. Answer (a): "New immigrants in Brazil are facing difficult living conditions, with many staying in overcrowded shelters that lack basic amenities. In the Brazilian state of Acre, a shelter meant for 200 people is housing around 1,300 foreigners waiting for visas to work in Brazil. The facility has few toilets and no showers, and is surrounded by mud. State officials have declared a "social emergency" in the area, and there are concerns about potential health crises, such as outbreaks of dengue, a mosquito-borne disease common in the region."  Answer (b): "Over 1,300 immigrants are waiting for visas to work in Brazil in a shelter meant for only 200 people. The living conditions are poor, with few toilets and no showers, and the shelter is surrounded by mud. The influx of immigrants has caused a "social emergency" in the town of Brasileia, and officials have declared a possible health crisis due to the lack of medical facilities and the potential for outbreaks of diseases such as dengue. Most of the immigrants have fled Haiti, but the route is also used by migrants from countries such as Senegal, Nigeria, the Dominican Republic, and even Bangladesh. Haitians receive special treatment from the Brazilian government, with 1,200 annual work visas granted to families from the Caribbean island. However, the number of visas proved to be much lower than the demand, and over the past 15 days, more than 1,300 Haitians crossed the border into Acre, more than the annual quota of visas. Non-Haitian immigrants face uncertainties and may have their requests denied. Despite the poor living conditions, many Brazilian companies are visiting the shelter to hire new workers."

Answer (a): "New immigrants in Brazil are facing difficult living conditions, with many staying in overcrowded shelters that lack basic amenities. In the Brazilian state of Acre, a shelter meant for 200 people is housing around 1,300 foreigners waiting for visas to work in Brazil. The facility has few toilets and no showers, and is surrounded by mud. State officials have declared a "social emergency" in the area, and there are concerns about potential health crises, such as outbreaks of dengue, a mosquito-borne disease common in the region."  Answer (b): "Over 1,300 immigrants are waiting for visas to work in Brazil in a shelter meant for only 200 people. The living conditions are poor, with few toilets and no showers, and the shelter is surrounded by mud. The influx of immigrants has caused a "social emergency" in the town of Brasileia, and officials have declared a possible health crisis due to the lack of medical facilities and the potential for outbreaks of diseases such as dengue. Most of the immigrants have fled Haiti, but the route is also used by migrants from countries such as Senegal, Nigeria, the Dominican Republic, and even Bangladesh. Haitians receive special treatment from the Brazilian government, with 1,200 annual work visas granted to families from the Caribbean island. However, the number of visas proved to be much lower than the demand, and over the past 15 days, more than 1,300 Haitians crossed the border into Acre, more than the annual quota of visas. Non-Haitian immigrants face uncertainties and may have their requests denied. Despite the poor living conditions, many Brazilian companies are visiting the shelter to hire new workers."

My answer:  UNICEF played a crucial role in India's successful eradication of polio by providing financial and technical support to the Indian government's National Polio Surveillance Project (NPSP) and setting up the Social Mobilisation Network for polio in 2001 in northern Uttar Pradesh state. The network worked with community mobilizers to promote hand-washing, hygiene, sanitation, exclusive breastfeeding, diarrhoea management, and routine immunization. UNICEF also partnered with the Indian government, Rotary clubs, WHO, and millions of front-line workers to implement innovative strategies and micro-plans to cover the last mile in the battle against polio.  Evaluation:  Both answers (a) and (b) provide accurate information about UNICEF's role in India's polio eradication efforts. However, answer (b) provides more details and context about UNICEF's work, such as the establishment of the Social Mobilisation Network for polio and the promotion of hand-washing, hygiene, sanitation, exclusive breastfeeding, diarrhoea management, and routine immunization. Additionally, answer (b) highlights UNICEF's partnership with various organizations and front-line workers, which further emphasizes their commitment to eradicating polio in India.  Therefore, Answer (b) is better.

My answer: UNICEF played a crucial role in India's successful eradication of polio through its Social Mobilisation Network for polio, which was established in response to resistance against the polio vaccine. The network worked with community mobilizers to promote hand-washing, hygiene, sanitation, exclusive breastfeeding, diarrhoea management, and routine immunization. UNICEF also partnered with the Indian government, Rotary clubs, WHO, and millions of front-line workers to implement innovative strategies and micro-plans to cover the last mile in the battle against polio. UNICEF continues to support India's efforts to ensure that children remain protected against polio until the disease is eradicated globally.

This additional information provides a clearer understanding of UNICEF's impact on the campaign.  Therefore, Answer (b) is better. Here's my answer:  The opening line-up of Manchester's new 25m arts centre Home includes a variety of exciting events and exhibitions. The centre's theatre will feature Stockport playwright Simon Stephens' adaptation of Hungarian writer Odon von Horvath's 1932 play Kasimir and Karoline, renamed as The Funfair. The play has also inspired an art exhibition featuring artists from Egypt, Iceland, and Kosovo, as well as Manchester. In addition, the centre will screen funfair-themed films, such as Big, Carnival of Souls, and Freaks. The opening line-ups are expected to provide more space to cater to Manchester's "hunger for new ideas" and boost the economy by attracting other businesses to its part of the city centre. The centre's artistic directors believe that it is an example of how arts can be a catalyst for regeneration. Overall, the opening line-ups at Home are expected to have a positive impact on Manchester's cultural scene and economy.

Here's my answer:  The new PNR directive has faced opposition and concerns from various groups, including the European Digital Rights (EDRI) campaign group, the EU Agency for Fundamental Rights (FRA), and critics of the directive. The EDRI group argues that the five-year data retention period is too long, and the directive may not prevent discriminatory profiling of individuals. The FRA questions the effectiveness of PNR collection for crime prevention and emphasizes the need for statistical analysis to detect discrimination and measure the importance of PNR in crime investigations. Critics suggest that the EU should focus on improving intelligence-sharing between police forces, and that PNR was irrelevant in the Paris and Brussels bombings as the jihadists did not fly to Europe to carry out their attacks. Additionally, privacy concerns include the European Court of Justice (ECJ) striking down an EU data retention directive in 2014, violating fundamental privacy rights, and the PNR data including sensitive information such as race, religion, and sexual life. To address these concerns, PIUs will be obliged to delete any such data if they receive it to avoid infringing EU anti-discrimination law, and there will be no automatic data transfer from PIUs to comply with EU data protection legislation.  Overall, while both answers (a) and (b) provide a summary of the concerns and opposition, answer (b) provides a more comprehensive and detailed summary, making it the better answer.

Here's my answer:  The new PNR directive has faced opposition and concerns from various groups, including the European Digital Rights (EDRI) campaign group and the EU Agency for Fundamental Rights (FRA). EDRI argues that the five-year data retention period is too long, and the directive may not prevent discriminatory profiling of individuals, and it will be an ineffective tool against terrorism. FRA questions the effectiveness of PNR collection for crime prevention and emphasizes the need for statistical analysis to detect any discrimination and measure the importance of PNR in crime investigations. Additionally, critics suggest that the EU should focus on improving intelligence-sharing between police forces, and that PNR was irrelevant in the Paris and Brussels bombings as the jihadists did not fly to Europe to carry out their attacks. Privacy concerns also arise as the PNR data includes sensitive information such as race, religion, and sexual life, and PIUs will be obliged to delete any such data if they receive it to avoid infringing EU anti-discrimination law. Moreover, there will be no automatic data transfer from PIUs to comply with EU data protection legislation.  In conclusion, the new PNR directive has faced opposition and concerns from various groups due to its potential infringement on privacy rights, questionable effectiveness in preventing terrorism, and the need for statistical analysis to measure its importance in crime investigations.

Here's my answer:  The new PNR directive has faced opposition and concerns from various groups, including the European Digital Rights (EDRI) campaign group, the EU Agency for Fundamental Rights (FRA), and critics of the directive. The EDRI group argues that the five-year data retention period is too long, the directive may not prevent discriminatory profiling of individuals, and it will be an ineffective tool against terrorism, as PNR was irrelevant in the Paris and Brussels bombings. The FRA questions the effectiveness of PNR collection for crime prevention and emphasizes the need for statistical analysis to detect any discrimination and measure the importance of PNR in crime investigations. Critics suggest that the EU should focus on improving intelligence-sharing between police forces, and that PNR was irrelevant in the Paris and Brussels bombings as the jihadists did not fly to Europe to carry out their attacks. Additionally, privacy concerns include the inclusion of sensitive information in PNR data, the need to delete such data to avoid infringing EU anti-discrimination law, and the lack of automatic data transfer from PIUs to comply with EU data protection legislation.  Therefore, it is important to consider these concerns and opposition when evaluating the effectiveness and implications of the new PNR directive.

Here's my own answer:  The number of UK fire and rescue staff taking long-term sick leave due to mental illness has risen by nearly a third over the last six years, with the figure doubling in London since 2011-12. Data from two-thirds of fire services revealed that the number of firefighters and other staff taking long-term mental health leave rose from 600 to 780 over the last six years. At least 126 staff have left the service due to mental health issues since 2011. In 2016-17, 97 Scottish fire staff took long-term mental health sick leave, while in Northern Ireland that figure was 111. A survey of over 1,600 staff and volunteers across emergency services shows nearly nine in 10 have experienced stress, low mood or poor mental health while working in their current role, and 25% of them had contemplated suicide. Here's my answer:  Deep sea mining has the potential to extract large quantities of valuable metals from the ocean floor, including gold, copper, manganese, cobalt, and other metals. The Clarion-Clipperton Zone in the eastern Pacific is a key area of interest, with an estimated 27 billion tonnes of mineral-rich nodules containing seven billion tonnes of manganese, 340 million tonnes of nickel, 290 million tonnes of copper, and 78 million tonnes of cobalt. The United Nations' International Seabed Authority is working on managing the extraction of these nodules and sharing the proceeds with developing countries.  The prospect of deep sea mining has sparked debate among marine scientists, with concerns about the potential environmental impact and the need for sustainable mining practices. However, proponents argue that deep sea mining could provide a valuable source of minerals, while also creating jobs and boosting local economies. As the reality of the first mining operations comes closer, it's important to continue the discussion on the moral and environmental implications of this emerging industry.
